Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ William Farrell; Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ 38 E ♥ The location of the capital Tabriz.

♠ Original name ♣ Il-khanate ♥

♠ Alternative names ♣ Il-Khanate; Il-Khanids; House of Hulegu ♥ [1] House of Hulegu.[2]

♠ Peak Date ♣ 1316 CE ♥

During the reign of Ḡāzān the Il-khanate entered "a new and dynamic era" [3]

However, it was Abu Said, whose reign began in 1316 CE, who "ruled during what was described as the 'best period of the domination of the Mongols'. The economy boomed, a treaty was negotiated with the Mamluks and Persia looked forward to peace and prosperity."[4]

The Ilkhanate "fell without in any real sense having previously declined. Why was this? ... The crucial reason is a simple one: Abu Said left no heir. ... the direct line of Hulegu had failed. ... None was able to gain control of the whole Ilkanate legacy. Of the factions the most notable, ultimately, were the Jalayirids, who built up a strong position in Iraq and Azabaijan which survived into the fifteenth century."[5]


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 1256-1339 CE ♥

Beginning 1256 CE. "By 1256, Hülegü [the Mongol commander] had all but eliminated the Ismāʿilis as an independent force in Persia (although individual forts remained independent for some time, even years), and had moved with the bulk of his army to Azerbaijan, which was to become the center of the Il-khanid state."[6]

End 1335 CE. The Ilkhanate came to an end with the death of Abu Sa'id in 1335 CE.[7] Dynastic failure: the Ilkhanate "fell without in any real sense having previously declined. Why was this? ... The crucial reason is a simple one: Abu Said left no heir. ... the direct line of Hulegu had failed."[8]

Actually the 1335 dates seems to be an over simplification. The actual end seems to be 1339 CE when Iran was divided into the four kingdoms: "who then set up another puppet, Sulayman Khan, a descendant of Hulagu, and gave him Sati Beg in marriage, while Hasan 'the Greater' set up as a rival a descendant of Abaqa named Shah Jahan Timur. A battle took place ... 1340 ... Hasan 'the Greater' was defeated ... deposed his puppet ... proclaiming himself king founded the dynasty ... of the Jala'irs, who reigned until 1411 over Western Persia and Mesopotamia with Baghdad as their capital. As for Hasan 'the Less,' ... he was murdered in 1343, while marching to attack his rival, by his wife ... The Mongol ascendancy in Persia was now at an end, and, until Timur's hordes swept over the country (1384-1393), it was divided into at least four kingdoms, those of the Jala'irs, the Muzaffaris, the Kurts, and the Sar-ba-dars..."[9]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state ♥ Ḡāzān had princes removed from power. [10]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ nominal allegiance; alliance ♥

Nominal allegiance

The Il-khans "never gave up their de jure recognition of the Great Khan’s preeminence."[11] Hülegü's succesors were officially invested by the Great Khan Qubilai; the Great Khan had a high commissioner at the court of the Il-khanid. [12]

Alliance - did this attempt result in any joint activity at all?

attempt was made to form alliance by crusaders and Mongols against Mamluks in Syria. However, "The problems of distance and the difficulties of synchronization proved, in thirteenth century conditions, to be insurmountable."[13]
Hulegu in Syria had the assistance of "16,000 Christian crusaders sent by King Hayton of Armenia."[14]

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Seljuk Empire ♥ Seljuk Kingdom of Rum; the Nezari Esmailis; Abbasid caliphate. the Nezāri Esmāʿilis = 'the Assassins'. The Il-Khanid state covered territory that had been ruled by several different polities from the Saljuq kingdom of Rum to the Nezāri Esmāʿilis ('the Assassins')to the Abbasid caliphate.[15]
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ cultural assimilation; elite migration ♥ "Hülegü took with him an enormous army, supposedly two out of every ten Mongol soldiers, who were accompanied by families and herds. This, then, was not just a military campaign but also the mass migration of a large portion of the Mongol nation to Persia and the surrounding countries." [16]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Chobanids ♥ "Although at first the Chobanids maintained the fiction that they were vassals of the ruling house of Hülegü (Hūlāgū), after the collapse of Il-khanid authority they became effectively independent rulers of the areas that they were able to seize."[17]
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Mongolian ♥ The Il-Khans and their armies were Mongolian and the Il-Khanid dynasty continued to recognise the authority of the Great Khan. [18]
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ 25,000,000 ♥ km squared. The area of the Mongolian Empire when it was split into its subdivisions of the Golden Horde, the Chagatai Khanate , Great Yuan and the Ilkhanate (purple), c. 1300. Calculated with Google Maps Area Calculator and map (below).

♠ Capital ♣ Maragha; Tabriz; Sultaniya ♥ Hulegu made Maragha the capital. This location, in Azarbaijan, had the best pastureland. The Ilkhans lived under tents.[19][20] Tabriz became the the capital 1281 CE.[21] Capital moved to Sultaniya 1313 CE: "Founded in about 1285 by Arghun, the sixth Ilkhanid ruler of Persia, who was attracted by its abundant pastures and used it as his summer capital, Sultaniya became the seat of empire under his son Mohammed Oljeytu Khudabanda in 1313."[22] Tabriz "developed into a great metropolis" but was not a capital.[23]

♠ Language ♣ Mongolian; Persian; Arabic ♥ The original Ilkhans were Mongols and therefore spoke Mongolian. Manuscripts were written in Persian and Arabic. [24] ♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥ Mongolian has been proposed as a member of the Altaic family, although the status of Altaic remains disputed. Persian is a member of the Pan-European family Persian is a part of the Indo-European family. [25]

♠ Religion Genus ♣ Buddhism: 1256-1294CE; Islam: 1295-1353CE ♥

♠ Religion Family ♣ Sunni: 1295-1306CE; Shii: 1307-1316CE; Sunni: 1316-1353CE ♥ "Early in his reign, Öljeytü dedicated the city of Solṭāniya in Azerbaijan, a project that had been initiated by Arḡun. Öljeytü built his mausoleum there, one of the pinnacles of Il-khanid architecture in Persia. In the winter of 1307-08 he declared his allegiance to the Shiʿites, and during the following years he made efforts to enforce this belief among the Muslims of his kingdom. This “conversion” certainly fueled the enmity of the Mamluks, and perhaps Öljeytü’s adoption of this faith was indirectly influenced by this ongoing conflict. Some Mamluk sources report that Öljeytü renounced his Shiʿite faith towards his death, but this is probably no more than wishful thinking on their (and their patrons’) part."[26]

General Description[edit]

The Ilkhanate was a state that began under Mongol commander Hulegu who founded the House of Hulegu.[28] The nearly eighty years the dynasty lasted was a time of general economic prosperity for the 5 million inhabitants of Persia. The end of the Ilkhanate came when Abu Said, who it is said "ruled during what was described as the 'best period of the domination of the Mongols".[29], died without an heir, which resulted in the Jalayirids becoming the strongest faction in the region.[30]

The Mongol invaders assimilated to the local culture in Persia. They converted to Islam, used the local languages (Persian and Arabic), and maintained existing Persian administrative practices, the financing of which was underpinned by iqta land grants awarded to senior bureaucrats and army officers.[31][32] According to the Persian historian Rashid al-Din, who was chief minister to Ghazan[33], the Mongols assessed the vizier (chief of the bureaucracy) on his ability to extract revenue.[34] Even so, previously better known in the region as barbarians bent on destruction, the Mongols rebuilt many hospitals, mosques, and observatories, and impressive mausoleums to the rulers appeared in the cities.[35][36]

During this period, Sultaniya was a famous commercial center and after the intense building activities of Oljetu (r.1304-1316 CE) the 'great city' became the capital. As a result of the work, the circumference of the outer walls almost tripled in length, containing within new fabulous palaces, gardens, and a purpose-built quarter of a thousand houses.[37] The largest city in the Ilkhanate at this time was probably Tabriz which also "developed into a great metropolis".[38] Tabriz had a cistern for drinking water and baths with hot water.[39] In 1300 CE Tabriz may have contained 100,000-200,000 inhabitants.

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 3,800,000 ♥ in squared kilometers. Estimate of Ilkhanate at its greatest extent.

The Ilkhanate emerged from the Mongol conquest of Iran, Iraq and Turkey. The armies of Hulegu had attacked and destroyed many of the great cites of the Middle East, and ended the Abbasid Caliphate. Local dynasties had to submit to Mongol rule. In 1300 the Mongols spilt up their empire into four semi-independent regions, one of these was the Ilkhanate. From then on it was a fairly autonomous polity, although its rulers acknowledged a higher authority in the Great Khan. The Ilkhanate saw the return of stability to the region. Over time the Ilkhanate absorbed influences from Persia and the Middle East, becoming less 'Mongol'.[41]


♠ Polity Population ♣ 5,250,000: 1300 CE ♥ People.

McEvedy and Jones[42]

Iran: 3.5m
Afghanistan: 1.75m

"The problem in Persia is that land that has been neglected may well not be easy to bring back into cultivation. Agriculture was, in the absence of large rivers or adequate rainfall, very dependent on the qanat system of underground water channels. ... qanats require constant skilled maintenance if they are to continue to operate, many will have been ruined, not necessarily by deliberate destruction but simply through long-term neglect because the peasants had fled. ... Similarly in the case of Iraq. ... It is unlikely that Hulegu deliberately destroyed the agricultural potential of Iraq though here, too, much damage could inadvertently have been done simply through lack of proper maintenance of the irrigation canals."[43]

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ [100,000-200,000]: 1300 CE ♥ Inhabitants. Estimate is more than Rayy in 1220 CE, significantly more on basis that Tabriz was likely the most populous city in Persia at this time.

Tabriz.

Prior to the Mongol conquest Rayy, in northern Iran, had about 80,000 in 1220 CE.[44]

Tabriz "developed into a great metropolis".[45] As the major trade center in Persia it therefore was likely the most populous city in Persia in the post-Mongol conquest era. Tabriz had a cistern for drinking water and baths with hot water.[46]

The population of Tabriz was larger than that of the later Ilkhan capital Sultaniya, which was also impressive.[47]

"Sultaniya was an important commercial centre, a 'great city', as Clavijo reported on his arrival on 26 June 1404 ... Founded in about 1285 by Arghun, the sixth Ilkhanid ruler of Persia, who was attracted by its abundant pastures and used it as his summer capital, Sultaniya became the seat of empire under his son Mohammed Oljeytu Khudabanda in 1313. The city was expanded aggressively, the outer walls increasing from twelve thousand paces in circumference to thirty thousand. ... Oljeytu intended Sultaniya to become a fully functioning capital, no mere royal camp. He duly embarked on a terrific building spree, ordering his courtiers to design graceful palaces and gardens. The vizier Rashid al-din built an entire quarter of a thousand houses. Another, Tak al-din Ali Shah, built a lavish, ten-thousand-dinar palace called Paradise, its doors, walls and floors studded with pearls, gold, rubies, turquoise, emeralds and amber. A city of monuments made from baked brick, stone and wood sprang up on the desert plain, luxuriously decorated with bronze doors, inlaid window grilles, marble revetments and mosaic faience."[48]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ 2000: 1256-1280 CE; 2100: 1281-1312 CE; 1800: 1313-1335 CE ♥ in kilometers.

Maragha (capital 1256-1280 CE) - Balkh: 2000km

Tabriz (capital 1281-1312 CE) - Balkh: 2100km

Sultaniya (capital 1313-1335 CE) - Balkh: 1800km

"Mostawfī distinguishes in his account of the revenues of the different provinces and districts between the revenues of certain towns and their surrounding districts (welāyāt) ... Among the towns and districts described in this way, all of which were situated on the main trade routes, were Baghdad, Kūfa, Wāseṭ, Ḥella, Isfahan, Solṭānīya, Qazvīn, Qom, Kāšān, Hamadān, Yazd, Tabrīz, Ojān, Ahar, Šūštar, Āva, Sāva, Zanjān, Marāḡa, and Shiraz." [50]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ 6 ♥ levels.


1. Khan
2. Royal household and the high officials e.g. chief minster, tax officials. Later, some were granted land holdings to support them.[51]


_Central government_

"For all these dynasties - whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate - there was a military affairs department (dīvān al-ǰayš, dīvān al-ʿarż/ʿāreż) in the central administration, headed by an official, normally a civilian, called the ṣāḥeb al-ǰayš or ʿāreż."[52]

2. wazir[53]
Persian historian Rashid al-Din was chief minister to Ghazan.[54] According to Rashid al-Din, the Mongols assessed the wazir on his ability to extract revenue.[55]
3. divans - Departments of state.
3. ṣāḥeb-e dīvān (postal service?)
"These stations were in the charge of the ṣāḥeb-e dīvān, who had nāʾebs in the provinces."[56]
4. nāʾebs
5. Station master
"Each station was to be run by a station master and to keep twenty horses for the government couriers"[57]
6. Stable hand inferred


_Provincial government_

2. Regional governors.
Members of the Khan's military retinue were appointed as regional governors to administer conquered territory. [58]


Local tax-farmers. "Apart from the shortage of liquid funds, because the Mongols had no officials capable of running the fiscal administration at the local level, it was convenient to place the responsibility for the provincial tax administration on the tax-farmers; and so the tax farm or moqāṭaʿa became the dominant fiscal institution of the Il-khanate... The tax-farmers were mainly local people—merchants, landowners, members of the bureaucracy, and amirs temporarily resident in the district. It was rare for them to be members of the Mongol military classes, perhaps partly because local people could be more easily coerced by the central government. " [59]


♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥ levels.

Until Ghazan the Reformer the Ilkhans were pagan[60] although Teguder had converted to Islam before 1284 CE.[61]

1. The Khan. After Ghazan's formal conversion to Islam he adopted the title padishah-i Islam which "expressed independence within the Mongol tradition and claims to pre-eminence in the Islamic world" [62]

2. Islamic judges. They were regulated under Ghazan's administrative reforms. [63]
3. Imams


♠ Military levels ♣ 6 ♥ levels.

"Noteworthy was the decimal chain of command, the grouping of soldiers in tens, hundreds, and thousands, up to an army division of 10,000 men (Mongolian tümän, Pers. tūmān), which was to have an enduring impact on the military organization of succeeding eastern Islamic powers, being adopted by, e.g., the Mughals in India."[64]


1. Khan.

The military retinue, governors, warriors holding iqtas and the tribes all owed military obligations to the Khan. [65]
2. Military retinue.
Like the Seljuks and the Ottomans, the Khans had “a group of armed, mainly free men (the majority of them foreigners), who served on a voluntary basis and were attached personally to the leader. They were his closet companions, friends and servants; they commanded the troops in wars, while a select group of them served as his bodyguard. Their livelihood was secured by their masters, predominantly from the booty acquired during incursions and wars. The strength of these retinues ranged from a few dozen to 3,000 men. When the founders of the new states began to transform their personal might into territorial power, they relied heavily on their military retinues, delegating them to and settling them on the territories they controlled. In this Gefolgschaft-type of state, it is the military retinue to which the origins of the formal institutions of power can be traced back.” [66]
2. Chief hajeb or espahsalar (commander) (10,000s?)
"For all these dynasties—whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate ... The commander-in-chief of the actual troops was normally a Turk, and held the title of “chief ḥāǰeb” (ḥāǰeb-e bozorg, ḥāǰeb al-ḥoǰǰāb, etc.) or espahsālār, lesser commanders having the unqualified title of ḥāǰeb."[67]
3. hajeb (lesser commander) (1,000s?)
4. intermediate officer (100s?) inferred
5. iqta holders (10s?)
Warriors holding iqtas. [68]
6. Soldiers
- those men who had to fight through tribal obligations or recruited locally.[69]

Professions[edit]

There were full time officers and soldiers within the Khan's military retinue and those warriors who held iqtas [land grants] in return for military service. [70] There were full time Muslim priests.

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ inferred present ♥ original code: absent: 1256-1303 CE; {present; absent}: 1304-1335 CE Changed on the basis of this: There were full time officers and soldiers within the Khan's military retinue and those warriors who held iqtas [land grants] in return for military service. [71]

absent

"the Ilkhan Ghazan decided to give a modest level of pay to low-ranking soliders, while the high-ranking Mongol officers remained unpaid."[72] In 1303 an officer captured by the Mamluks reportedly said: "The Mongol is the slave of his sovereign, He is never free. His sovereign is his benefactor: he does not serve him for money. Although I was the least of Ghazan's servants I never needed anything."[73]

present

Senior warriors within the Khan's retinue. [74]

There were full time officers and soldiers within the Khan's military retinue and those warriors who held iqtas [land grants] in return for military service. [75] There were full time Muslim priests.

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent: 1256-1303 CE; present: 1304-1335 CE ♥

Originally the Ilkhanid army was a branch of the Mongol army, organised on a tribal basis. The Ilkhans did not adopt the slave-soldier system; instead maintain "large armies of mounted horse archers (supplemented by infantry and cavalry auxiliaries" from local rulers.[76]

After "the Ilkhanate was no longer expanding and the supply of plunder could not be relied upon" Ghazan the Reformer introduced the iqta system of land grants to pay soldiers.[77][78]

However, it is uncertain "to what extent the distribution of iqtas was actually implemented, for this particular edict was issued only very shortly before Ghazan's early death in 703/1304."[79] More so because our source for the Ilkhanate's apparently successful use of the itqa system is Rashid al-Din who was Ghazan's great minister and the individual responsible for implementing the policy.[80]

In any case, Ghazan's reforms may have put the financing of the army on a better footing, so that eventually warriors were being allocated iqtas [grants of land holding] to provide revenues with which to equip themselves.[81]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ e.g. imams.

Buddhists. Nestorian and Jacobite Christians.[82]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ e.g. chief minster, tax officials. [83] There were full time officials involved in administration at the royal court and regional governors as well. The bureaucracy's main focus was on collecting revenues and providing writing services. Senior officials were granted land holdings to support their offices. Appointments were made by the Khan, it was not a meritocratic service. [84]

♠ Source of support ♣ Land; state salary ♥

Officials such as Šams-al-Dīn Jovaynī and Rašīd-al-Dīn had landed estates which provided them with revenues. [85]

"For all these dynasties—whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate" the ṣāḥeb al-ǰayš or ʿāreż "kept the pay rolls and paid out salaries". [86]

♠ Examination system ♣ inferred absent ♥ Appointments were made by the Khan, it was not a meritocratic service.[87]

♠ Merit promotion ♣ inferred absent ♥ Appointments were made by the Khan, it was not a meritocratic service.[88]

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ e.g royal treasury. [89]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥ Islamic law was acknowledged by the Khans, and Islamic judges were regulated under Ghazan's administrative reforms. [90]

♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ Islamic judges, qadis, who were regulated under Ghazan's administrative reforms. [91][92]

♠ Courts ♣ inferred present ♥ The institutions that Islamic judges presided over. [93]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥ e.g. caravansaries, some built within cities. Or the baths that every city was ordered to build by Ghazan [94] The Mongols were famously nomadic and during campaigns destroyed many fortress and cities. It is not clear that the Il-Khanids become wholly sedentary. Evidence suggests they maintained some nomadic practices. "We have an indication that the later tribesmen maintained the same pastoral nomadic lifestyle as their forefathers... Until late in the Il-khanate period there is evidence that the royal family and the court in general maintained a peripatetic lifestyle, moving between winter and summer camps." [95] However, they did begin re-building in Persia following the destruction of the original Mongolian invasion. Their palaces and other similar buildings were large and elaborately decorated.[96] "From the beginning of their regime, the Mongols were patrons of high culture." [97] This included architecture: hospitals, mosques, observatories and mausoleum were all built by members of the dynasty and the higher civil servants.

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Hospital at Amasya. [98] In Iraq, Ala al-Din Juwayni oversaw the construction of a new city "al-Ma'man ... equipped with a Friday Mosque, a diwan, a market and a school."[99] Baths with hot water in Tabriz.[100]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ qanats in Persia and irrigation canals in Iraq. however, under the Mongols many fell into disrepair and disuse.[101] Arch dams were constructed e.g. at Kibar (Kivar): "the dam is 85 ft. high and 180 ft. long at the crest, the thickness of which is between 15 and 16 1/2 ft."[102] A gravity dam was built at Sawa.[103]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ Cistern for drinking water in Tabriz.[104]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ General reference for Seljuk? - Safavid? time period: "The bāzār was usually, though not always, divided into a number of sūqs (markets) in which different crafts and occupations had separate quarters. At night, after members of the crafts and shopkeepers had shut their premises and retired to their homes, the gates of the bāzārs were locked and barred."[105] In Iraq, Ala al-Din Juwayni oversaw the construction of a new city al-Ma'man which was provided with a market.[106]
♠ Food storage sites ♣ present ♥ General reference for Seljuk? - Safavid? time period: "Caravansaries, where goods were unloaded on arrival and where merchants could take rooms, were to be found both in or close to the bāzārs and on the outskirts of the city."[107]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥ Taj al-Din ‘Ali Shah, colleague of Rashid al-Din, had a mosque built at Tabriz, capital of the Ilkhanid. Oljeitu, who succeeded Ghazan, had a tomb built at Sultaniyya [108]

Oljeitu's stone architecture in Sultaniyya: "domed and octagonal buildings, many of which were architectural masterpieces - none more so than his own tomb, constructed in 1313."[109] Monumental octagonal tomb of Oljeytu was 120 feet diameter. Tomb complex "incorporated a mosque, madrassah, hospital and khanaqah (a hostel for travelling dervishes) ... one of the grandest pious endowments of its time."[110] Blair says that the most impressive buildings built by the Il-khans were their tombs. The tomb of Sultan Oljāytu/Öljeytü at Solṭāniya is "the best surviving example". [111] The dome is "one of the largest uninterrupted spaces of medieval times, and the subtle interpenetration of volumes make this building a landmark of world architecture in which the sultan’s desire for immortality was deftly realized". [112]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ ♥ unknown

"For all these dynasties—whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate" the ṣāḥeb al-ǰayš or ʿāreż, among other duties, "organized weapon-training and equestrian games and exercises like polo, lance-thrusting at targets, etc.". [113]

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥ Ilkhanate libraries read Celeste (2016) available online.[114]

Library and archives in Tabriz.[115]

Hülegü paid for the construction of an astronomical observatory at Marāḡa. [116]

Ala al-Din Juwayni oversaw the construction of a new city al-Ma'man which was provided with a school.[117]

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ Repairs of roads.[118]
♠ Bridges ♣ present ♥ It is not stated in the sources that the Ilkhans destroyed all the bridges in Persia and this would be unlikely due to the importance of bridges to trade. Ghazan tried to improve security on the roads[119] and may have built or maintained bridges.
♠ Canals ♣ present ♥ Repairs of canals.[120]In Iraq, Ala al-Din Juwayni (Ilkhan official, governor of Baghdad) "built a canal from the Euphrates town of Anbar to Kufa and Najaf in an effort to promote agricultural production and allegedly led to the creation of 150 villages along the bank."[121]
♠ Ports ♣ present ♥ e.g. Basra.

The most impressive/costly building(s)[edit]

♠ height ♣ {50; 75} ♥ in m. Dome was 50 meters high. [122] "The Mausoleum of Oljeitu ... its cupola stands over 75m (nearly 250 feet) above the ground and is decorated with the most dazzling blue ceramic tiles."[123]
♠ extent ♣ 20 ♥ in m (the horizontal dimension along the longest axis). Longest axis of the rectangular main hall. [124]
♠ cost ♣ ♥ in people-years. Monumental octagonal tomb of Oljeytu was 120 feet diameter. Tomb complex "incorporated a mosque, madrassah, hospital and khanaqah (a hostel for travelling dervishes) ... one of the grandest pious endowments of its time."[125] Mamluk biographer al Yusufi said "Five thousand moved dirt, and five thousand cut and dressed stone. There were five thousand wagons to move rock and other materials, for which there were ten thousand donkeys. They made a thousand kilns for brick, and a thousand kilns for lime. Five thousand camels transported wood, and two thousand persons were assigned to cut wood from the mountains and other places. Three thousand smiths were employed to work sheets of metal, windows, nails and the like. There were five hundred carpenters, and five thousand men laid marble. Supervisors were appointed over them to urge them on in the work."[126]

Information[edit]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ Ghazan the Reformer standardized the weights and measures.[130] "the gold and silver coins and the measures (kila, gas) were standardised according to the standards of Tabriz".[131] Mathematics used "chord lengths" in trigonometry. [132]
♠ Area ♣ present ♥ Ghazan the Reformer standardized the weights and measures.[133] "the gold and silver coins and the measures (kila, gas) were standardised according to the standards of Tabriz".[134] There were a variety of weights and measures in use in commerce and in the coinage; Gazan made some efforts to enforce uniformity. There may not have been a permanent success however.[135]
♠ Volume ♣ present ♥ Ghazan the Reformer standardized the weights and measures.[136] "the gold and silver coins and the measures (kila, gas) were standardised according to the standards of Tabriz".[137]
♠ Weight ♣ present ♥ Ghazan the Reformer standardized the weights and measures.[138] "the gold and silver coins and the measures (kila, gas) were standardised according to the standards of Tabriz".[139]
♠ Time ♣ present ♥ The Mongols used “12-year animal cycle for dating purposes.” [140]
♠ Geometrical ♣ present ♥ Geometric algebra used in mathematics. [141]
♠ Other ♣ ♥

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ e.g. the writings of Rašid al-Din one of the main sources for Mongolian history as well as the Il-Khans. [142] This period is seen as one of flourishing production of illustrated manuscripts sponsored by the court and senior officials. At first Baghdad was the centre of production, before it moved to Tabriz after the court converted to Islam. A Chinese influence is clearly seen on manuscript illustration at this time.[143]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ e.g. Persian, Arabic. The administration used Persian writing. Arabic manuscripts survive as well [144] Both Persian and Arabic were in use, and a wide range of document types and scholarly subjects were produced.
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ absent ♥
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ present ♥

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ present ♥ Tables of cities with their latitudes and longitudes used in astronomy. [145]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ e.g. Abu Rayḥān Biruni’s Ketāb al-āṯār al-bāqia ʿan al-qorun al-ḵālia ( “The Chronology of Ancient Nations”) contains calendars. [146]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ present ♥ e.g. Qu'ran. Öljeytü commissioned a "gigantic Koran" from fine artists. [147]
♠ Religious literature ♣ present ♥ After the official conversion of the court to Islam there was "a specific Il-khanid interest in patronizing works [illustrated manuscripts] that deal with different religions of the past and present, emphasizing the prominence of Islam above all the others and in particular of Shiʿite Islam" [148]
♠ Practical literature ♣ present ♥ In the 11th century and after "the genre of writing treatises on statecraft in Persian develops, such treatises usually containing advice on the organizing of armies and on the art of war."[149] Treatise on government finance written by the scholar Tusi. [150]
♠ History ♣ present ♥ The History of the Mongols (Tāriḵ-e ḡāzāni-e mobārak) written by Rašid al-Din, who was Gazan's head administrator and eventually co-vizier. [151] Chronicler Abul Qasim Kashani.[152] Persian historian Rashid Al-Din.[153]
♠ Philosophy ♣ present ♥ Philosopher and astronomer Nasir al-Din Tusi.[154]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ present ♥ e.g. "a translation from Arabic into Persian of a zoological text on the usefulness of organs and other body parts of animals (Manāfeʿ-e ḥayawān) ordered by Ḡāzān Khan" [155] Hulegu built an astronomical observatory for polymath Nasir al-Din Tusi.[156]
♠ Fiction ♣ present ♥ "Poetry, painting and ceramics, but above all architecture, all flourished under the Mongols."[157]

Money[edit]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ e.g. textiles, animals. Used in barter and tribute. [158] A variety of money was used in the Ilkhanate. Articles were used in barter. Coins existed alongside articles. These included gold and silver coins, coins minted by the Ilkhante and foreign coins acquired in commerce and through tribute from other rulers. Money leading associations show the existence of debt and credit among the population. The Ilkhanate was part of the Mongols extensive postal system [Yam system] that carried royal communications around the empire.
♠ Tokens ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Precious metals ♣ present ♥ e.g. gold and silver in use in the region. [159]
♠ Foreign coins ♣ present ♥ Through trade and through tribute, for example from the Seljuks who paid partly in cash. [160]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ present ♥ Dinars.[161] Ghazan the Reformer reformed the coinage.[162] "the gold and silver coins and the measures (kila, gas) were standardised according to the standards of Tabriz".[163]
♠ Paper currency ♣ present ♥ Geikhatu’s minster Sadr al-Din issued paper money along Chinese lines the chao, following concerns about the lack of funds in the royal treasury. These paper certificates had Chinese character printed on them and the Muslim confession of faith. [164]
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ Money-lending associations, known as ortaqs emerged in this period. Tax farmers paid part of the expected sum in advance or through bonds issued by merchants. [165]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ present ♥ e.g. Royal treasury [166]
♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ Mongols required non-fighting people to perform labour duties, including service in postal relays. [167]
♠ Postal stations ♣ present ♥ Post stations were erected at distances of one day's journey between them. Marco Polo said that this was 25-30 miles distance. [168] The extensive Yam system was used to communicate royal orders and royal envoys across the empire.[169] Ḡāzān Khan established "special yāms exclusively for official couriers (īḷčī or yārāltū; cf. Doerfer, III, p. 12 and I, pp. 551-53) and making each one the responsibility of a grand amir. Stations were built along main arteries at a distance of three farsaḵs from one another and were required to have on hand fifteen well-nourished (farbeh) horses at all times. Two special couriers (peyk) were stationed at each yām; their function was to carry to the capital important reports about the provinces; such reports bore a special seal called tamḡā-ye peykī (seal of the messenger). A single courier could travel 30 farsaḵs in twenty-four hours, changing mounts frequently; the distance could be doubled if relays were used. Theoreti­cally an urgent message could reach Tabrīz from Khorasan in four days (Rašīd-al-Dīn, Jāmeʿ al-tawārīḵ, Baku, pp. 483-84; idem, Tārīḵ-eḡāzānī, pp. 274-75; cf. Spuler, Mongolen3, pp. 424-25)."[170]
♠ General postal service ♣ absent ♥ "Although the Mongol postal service was a government operation, merchants and others also made use of it." However this practice was ended by Möngke (r.1251-1260 CE) who "gave clear orders that the couriers had to stay on their prescribed routes and execute their orders exactly."[171]
♠ Fastest individual communication ♣ 7: 1300 CE ♥ Both Marco Polo and Rashid al-Din mention express couriers who would travel 200 miles a day. A fresh horse would be provided at every post station. [172]

Tabriz-Balkh: 2100km. At 322 km per day, 6.5 days.

Hormuz - Sultaniya by camel: 60 days. "From China came pearls, mother of pearl and rubies, shipped to the port of Hormuz in southern Persia. Here they were expertly drilled and strung and exported 'to all parts of the western world'. Many were loaded onto camels for the sixty-day caravan journey to Sultaniya where they were bought by merchants 'from Christian lands' and from Turkey, Syria, and Baghdad." (quoting Abul Qasim Kashani).[173]

Animals used in warfare[edit]

♠ Dogs ♣ absent ♥
♠ Donkeys ♣ inferred present ♥ Pack animal? Donkeys were present. The 8th Ilkhan was "originally given the name Kharbandeh or 'Donkey herder' following Mongol tradition of naming a person for the first thing they see at birth, which out of respect to his Muslim subjects was changed to Khodabandeh or 'Servant of God'".</ref>George Lane. 2018. A Short History Of The Mongols. I.B. Tauris & Co. Ltd. London.</ref>
♠ Horses ♣ present ♥ As used by Mongol cavalry, the main fighting force. [191] As the Ilkhanid forces had emerged from the Mongols they fought in classic style of Steppe warfare. The core of the army was mounted cavalry, whose main weapon was the bow. Javelins and a variety of hand weapons were also used. Siege weapons used included large crossbows, mangonels and incendiary devices. Although some soldiers had metal armour, such as chain mail, other were more lightly equipped with leather and heavy padding.

Armor[edit]

♠ Wood, bark, etc ♣ present ♥ e.g. Willow-wood shields. [192]
♠ Leather, cloth ♣ present ♥ Helmets had flaps made of leather. Some Mongol armour was made of hide, which "consisted of six layers tightly sewn together and shaped, after being softened by boiling,to fit the body." [193]
♠ Shields ♣ present ♥ Willow-wood shields were carried by some soldiers. Tortoise shell shields used assaults on fortifications. [194]
♠ Helmets ♣ present ♥ Made of steel and leather. [195]
♠ Breastplates ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No reference to plate armour. Plate armour more typical for heavy cavalry which carried lances rather than horsemen with bows. However, some Mongol cavalry carried lances.[196]
♠ Limb protection ♣ present ♥ Arm defences made of flaps of metal armour. P.243 [197]
♠ Chainmail ♣ present ♥ Attached to helmets as neck protection only. [198]
♠ Scaled armor ♣ present ♥ Iron scaled body armour worn by some soldiers. [199]
♠ Laminar armor ♣ present ♥ Full-length lamellar cuirass of central Asian style shown in Ilk-Kanid manuscripts. [200]
♠ Plate armor ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No reference to plate armour. Plate armour more typical for heavy cavalry which carried lances rather than horsemen with bows. However, some Mongol cavalry carried lances.[201]

Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ absent: 1256-1303 CE; present: 1304-1335 CE ♥

Originally the Ilkhanid army was a branch of the Mongol army, organised on a tribal basis. The Ilkhans did not adopt the slave-soldier system, instead maintain "large armies of mounted horse archers (supplemented by infantry and cavalry auxiliaries" from local rulers.[210]

After "the Ilkhanate was no longer expanding and the supply of plunder could not be relied upon" Ghazan the Reformer introduced the iqta system of land grants to pay soldiers.[211][212]

However, it is uncertain "to what extent the distribution of iqtas was actually implemented, for this particular edict was issued only very shortly before Ghazan's early death in 703/1304."[213] More so because our source for the Ilkhanate's apparently successful use of the itqa system is Rashid al-Din who was Ghazan's great minister and the individual responsible for implementing the policy.[214]

♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ inferred absent ♥ As Mongol soldiers had to provide their own equipment. [215]

"For all these dynasties—whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate" the ṣāḥeb al-ǰayš or ʿāreż, among other duties, "held periodic inspections of weapons and mounts, weeded out from the ranks interlopers and substitutes". [216]

♠ Total army ♣ [200,000-300,000] ♥ Mamluk writer al-Umari said the number of soldiers on registers of the Ilkhanate was 200,000-300,000. Morgan says "It does not seem likely that such figures should be treated literally" although J.M. Smith jnr does. [217] Kelly De Vries writes that Hulagu’s army was “huge,” noting that “Marco Polo described these forces as numbering some 300,000 cavalry in 1260.” Although this number might be an exaggeration, it is worth nothing that Mongke had given Hugalu two out of every 10 Mongolian soldiers for the 1258-60 invasions, and supplemented these numbers with an even larger number of Kipchak Turks recruited from the steppe peoples west of Mongolia… De Vries also notes that based on the small garrisons left behind in the conquered cities, which constitute only a fraction of the larger army, we can infer the immense size of the Mongol military force: “Even if we accept that Marco Polo’s number of 300,000 was an exaggeration, the force which went with him, leaving only 10,000-12,000 [in the conquered cities], must have been substantial.” [218]

♠ Professional army ♣ suspected unknown ♥ How many soldiers were provided with iqtas? Mongol army characterized by a history of warrior tradition, all men between 15-60 were eligible for conscription and intensive training [219]

♠ Largest fielded army ♣ 80,000 ♥ In 1281 the army sent against the Mamluk Sultan numbered c, 80, 000 soldiers. Made up of 50, 00 Mongols and the rest a mixture of ethnicities. [220]

♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥

♠ Sources of recruits ♣ mass mobilization; domestic ♥ “All male Mongol adults below the age of 60 were liable for military service.” [221]

Originally the Ilkhanid army was a branch of the Mongol army, organised on a tribal basis. The Ilkhans did not adopt the slave-soldier system, instead maintain “large armies of mounted horse archers (supplemented by infantry and cavalry auxiliaries” from local rulers.[222]

Ghazan's reforms looked to put the financing of the army on a better footing, so that eventually warriors were being allocated iqtas [grants of land holding] to provide revenues with which to equip themselves. [223]

♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers ♥

Mongol soldiers would have provided their own horses. [224]

Qupchur levies were enforced to re-equip soldiers who were lacking horses or items - officers and soldiers with a surplus of horses or equipment had to give them up. These were then redistributed to others. [225]

by state?

"For all these dynasties — whose administrative infrastructures tended in any case to be derived from, or at least strongly influenced by, those of the ʿAbbasid Caliphate" - the ṣāḥeb al-ǰayš or ʿāreż, among other duties, "procured mounts and draught animals". [226]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Abbasid Caliphate, Ayyubid Syria, Mamluks, Chagatai Khanate, Golden Horde.

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ Ismaili Nizari Hashshashins.

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ "Only one chief minister during the entire period of the Ilkhanate died a natural death, a statistic which does not seem to have deterred aspirants to the office."[227] "In the first few years of his reign Ghazan executed numerous members of the House of Hulegu."[228] Berke vs Hulagu 1262 CE.

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ present ♥ "By the 1290s the Ilkhanate was going through a period of great internal unrest: the economy had virtually collapsed, foreign debts were not being met, and the cities were torn by riots and the threat of insurrection."[229]

♠ Military revolts ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ inferred present ♥ With the end of the House of Helugu "Persia drifted without a unified government".[230]

Cultural distance[edit]

♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ present ♥ Berke vs Hulagu 1262 CE.

♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ Arabic vs Mongolian/Persian.

♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ present ♥ Mongolic vs Indo-European, etc.

♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ present ♥ Ismaili Nizari Hashshashins. Ghazan (recent Muslim convert) vs Baydu (officially Muslim, tolerant of religions).

♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ present ♥ After conversion to Islam the Ilkhanids persecuted Christians "whose numbers were in consequence greatly diminished."[231] Anti-Jewish pogroms in Tabriz and Baghdad 1291 CE sparked by policies of Jewish vazir.[232]

♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ absent ♥

♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ absent ♥

♠ Wars on steppe frontier ♣ present ♥ Invaded by Chagatai Khanate.[233] Golden Horde.

♠ Wars on agricultural frontier ♣ absent ♥

Intensity of warfare[edit]

♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥

In 1300 CE Ghazan invaded Syria and "drove all the Mamluk forces back into Egypt."[234]

Oljeitu invaded Gilan, on the Caspian coast, and added it to Ilkhanate territories.[235]

♠ Looting ♣ present ♥

Raids into Syria gave the Mongol army "something useful and profitable to do if they were to avoid the dangers of faction and civil war in a period in which Mongol territory had ceased to expand."[236]

♠ Sack ♣ present ♥

Sack of Baghdad under Hulegu.[237]

♠ Rape ♣ present ♥

Sack of Baghdad 1258 CE. "They took Baghdad in a violent assault, followed by considerable slaughter and rape."[238]

♠ Deportation ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Expulsion ♣ present ♥

"There is no evidence that the shamans were expelled from the Ilkhanate after Ghazan's conversion, as were the bakhshis, the Buddhist holy men, who were eventually ordered out when it became clear that they were still practicing their religion while having ostensibly become Muslims. The shamans, on the other hand, are found voicing their opinions in Oljeitu's reign, and may have lingered in Iran for some time, although their eventual fate is unknown."[239]

♠ Enslavement ♣ present ♥

"the Mongols were only too keen to use enslaved peoples as expendable 'cannon fodder'".[240]

♠ Imprisonment ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Destruction ♣ present ♥

Sack of Baghdad 1258 CE destroyed the Grand Library, mosques, palaces and hospitals. "Hulegu's invasion had not, except in the case of the sack of Baghdad, been destructive to the extent of Chingiz Khan's. Presumably Hulegu knew well enough that he and his followers were coming to stay: there was little point in destroying their own potential property."[241]

♠ Torture ♣ present ♥

According to one scribe Christians tortured and forced to wear "symbols of opprobrium, a black linen over their shoulders, so that whoever saw them would recognize that they are Christians and would curse them".[242]

♠ Mutilation ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Targeted massacre ♣ present ♥

"In the first few years of his reign Ghazan executed numerous members of the House of Hulegu."[243]

♠ General massacre ♣ present ♥ 1258 CE Sack of Baghdad.

"Juwayni, a historian who was also a lifelong servant of the Mongols, has often been accused of flattering them. Yet he wrote that 'every town and every village has been several times subjected to pillage and massacre and has suffered this confusion for years' ... This was at the beginning of the Ilkhanate. At its end, Hamd Allah Mustawfi Qazwini, another Mongol government official, concluded that 'there is no doubt the destruction which happened on the emergence of the Mongol state and the general massacre that occurred at that time will not be repaired in a thousand years...'"[244]

♠ Extermination ♣ inferred present ♥ 1258 CE Sack of Baghdad.

♠ Ethnocide ♣ present ♥

After conversion to Islam the Ilkhanids persecuted Christians "whose numbers were in consequence greatly diminished."[245]

"Buddhist structures were destroyed or converted to Islamic use" after 1295 CE.[246]

"In the Ilkhanate, after Ilkhan Gazan converted to Islam around 1295, the Mongols persecuted and massacred the Christians in their realm. The Ilkhanate Mongols also destroyed Buddhist temples and synagogues. They forced those not following the Islamic faith to convert to Islam or leave the Ilkhanate."[247]

Ilkhanid-Syrian (1259 CE-1260 CE)

Ritual variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ Purcell responsible for most research and coding; Cioni responsible for additional research and coding, and general editing of section.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥ The name of the historical or archaeological expert who supervised coding and checked/improved/approved the result. If more than one expert was involved, list all.

Description of the Ritual System

The ritual system of the Ilkhanates is taken to be based on Sunni Islam. However, they were originally Buddhist rulers who after finding themselves to be ruling over a large Muslim population converted to Islam. They were almost certainly Sunni Muslims, however, there were suggestions that individual rulers might have converted or attempted to convert to Shi'i Islam under the influence of Shi'i advisors. [248]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Hajj ♥ "In the pilgrimage to Mecca, the hajj, ancient Arab rites have been taken over and spiritualized. The pilgrimage is performed during the last lunar month, Dhu'l­hijja. People who come to Arabia at other times can perform the lesser pilgrimage, umra, which consists mainly of the circumambulation of the Ka'ba and the running between two hillocks called Safa and Marwa. Pilgrimage to a sacred place requires a ritual consecration. Thus, at a certain distance from Mecca the men, with shaven heads, put on a special garment, ihram, consisting of two white unsewn pieces of cloth that cover their whole body; women put on a covering garment. Pilgrims greet the sacred precincts with the call labbaika, "Here I am at Thy service!" The rites that have to be performed include the sevenfold circumambulation of the Ka'ba (where one tries to kiss the black stone in its southeastern corner) and a sevenfold running between Safa and Marwa, which are now connected by covered arcades. But the central rite is the "staying" on the hill 'Arafat*, a distance of some eight miles from Mecca, on the ninth day of the month. There, sermons are given, and on the way back the 'stoning of Satan' is performed by casting seven pebbles three times at a certain place. After that, on the tenth day of the month, each pilgrim slaughters a sheep or larger animal in Mina. Those staying home do the same. This part of the ritual commemorates the day when Abraham was ordered to sacrifice his son Isma'il, whose place was then taken by a ram. After the Feast of Offering the pilgrim can return from the sacred state, ihram, into the normal state, when the cutting of hair and nails, as well as sex, are again permitted. Killing any creatures inside the sacred area is considered a major sin. The pilgrimage should be performed only when the pilgrim is in good health and can undertake the journey without major difficulties, especially without incurring debts; this shows the realistic approach of Islam to religious practice. In former centuries the pilgrimage was a dangerous undertaking for those who came from distant lands, whether they traveled overland in the caravans which started, with great pomp, in Cairo or Baghdad, or sailed from India or Malaysia on fragile boats. (Air travel is not always very safe either.) Yet modern Muslims sometimes express the view that these slow, arduous journeys prepared them better for the great event than the brief trip by air, which barely leaves leisure to meditate on the experience of visiting the center of their religion. On their return the pilgrims bring a vessel filled with wholesome water from the well Zamzam, from which Hagar once drank. They are received by their families with pride and joy, and are called Hajji. Those who die during the pilgrimage are regarded as martyrs, shahid, "witness" for their faith. Doubtlessly the meeting of people from all parts of the world in Mecca and their common worship at the central sanctuary of Islam does much to strengthen the feeling of unity among Muslims. Through the centuries many scholars and pious people extended their stay in Mecca and exchanged their religious views with colleagues from other parts of the world. The sojourn near the Ka'ba inspired important theological and mystical works: the comprehensive commentary on the Koran by Zamakhshari (called by the honorific title Jar Allah, "God's neighbor" due to his prolonged stay in Mecca) grew out of the author's stay in Mecca. Even more evident is the influence of the Meccan experience on the most comprehensive work of Islamic theosophy, the Futuhat al­makkiyya, of Ibn 'Arabi, which was "revealed" to the author while he was circumambulating the Ka'ba. Furthermore, it is important to remember that almost all reform movements in the fringe areas of the Islamic world were triggered off by the pilgrimage. Faithful believers from Bengal and Nigeria, from Morocco and Central Asia, who had lived for some time in Mecca and exchanged their views with others who, like them, had experienced what seemed to be the 'true Arabic Islam', returned to their Page 39countries to fight the numerous indigenous superstitions and customs which had infiltrated the Islam of the masses and which now appeared to them like remnants of ancient paganism."[249]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, although "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)".[250]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[251]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ The inhabitants of Mecca, Medina and other important waypoints along the pilgrimage may be considered as "passive spectators or witnesses", as the Seshat codebook puts it. They would witness the Hajj, or parts of it, between the tenth and the twelfth months of the Islamic calendar, every year.[252]
♠ Duration ♣ 144 ♥ hours. 6 days From the 8th to the 13th day of the last month of the Islamic calendar..[253] The main acts of the Hajj take place in five days of the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, that is, from the 8th to the 13th of the month of Dhu'l Hijja: however, "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)"[254]

Costs of participation
♠ currency ♣ present ♥ Although there was no official cost of participation, in that no money was charged for pilgrimage, to undertake hajj was an expensive occurrence which prohibited many from undertaking it. The pilgrim would need to travel to Mecca, would need food and accommodation for that time, would need money to buy animals for sacrifice..[281]
♠ property/valuable items ♣ present ♥ Though this is not prescribed by sacred texts, there is a long historical tradition of the wealthiest pilgrims distributing money and precious objects (e.g. textiles) to the less wealthy.[282]
♠ health costs ♣ present ♥ Hajj was physically demanding including a seven times repeated run between two hills and several miles trekking into the desert..[283]
♠ animal sacrifice ♣ present ♥ On the third of the five main days of the Hajj, animal sacrifice is performed.[284]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[288]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[289]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[290]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[291]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[292]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ Though no sources were found describing specifically how Nowruz was celebrated under the Ilkhanate, it is very likely that this was the most euphoric ritual celebrated in this polity, since it was very popular throughout the medieval Islamic world, particularly in polities influenced by Persian culture. "Of Persian origin, Nawruz (No Roz; new day), the ancient Iranian festival of the New Year, survived the Arab conquest and continued to be celebrated in Islamic lands many centuries thereafter. Originally the first day of the Persian solar year, it corresponded with the spring vernal equinox, and the duration of its celebration was six days. Medieval Islamic sources give various (folkloric) explanations for the origins of Nawruz, which involve the mythical Persian Jamshid or the biblical patriarchs Abraham and King Solomon. No Roz was the day to wear new clothes and serve food of the new season. People used to rise early in the morning, go to wells and streams, draw water in vases, and pour it over them- selves or sprinkle it on each other. Explanations for these customs varied, from considering them a good omen and a means to ward off harm to the cleansing of the smoke off of those attending the rituals of the fire of the preceding winter nights. Shi‘i explanation of the significance of Nawruz, which is ascribed to the Imam Ja‘far al-Sadiq, sees in it the day of God’s primal covenant with mankind, the day of the first rising of the sun, the day of the defeat of the Antichrist, and the day of a number of other important events." [362] The festival involved feasting, visiting relatives, exchanging gifts, music, dancing, singing, and various forms of entertainment [363].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Originally the first day of the Persian solar year, it corresponded with the spring vernal equinox, and the duration of its celebration was six days." [364]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "Originally the first day of the Persian solar year, it corresponded with the spring vernal equinox, and the duration of its celebration was six days." [365]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "Originally the first day of the Persian solar year, it corresponded with the spring vernal equinox, and the duration of its celebration was six days." [366]. (KB:Coded as 'not applicable' as quote suggests everyone participated)

♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Ruling elites and commoners alike celebrated this festival, and Muslims as well as non-Muslims [370][371]. (KB:Coded as 'not applicable' as quote suggests everyone participated)

♠ other ♣ present ♥ "It was precisely because Nowruz was associated from the outset with cultural memories of the splendor and divinely bestowed power of the royal courts of pre-Islamic Persia that it was attractive to rulers, from the Abbasid caliphs to the Pahlavis. Along with its many ceremonies, and most notably that of gift exchange, it provided the rulers with an alternative source of affirming and enhancing their power and prestige through a strictly non-Islamic channel; for unlike religious festivals, they could appear and be celebrated as the focal point and the peerless heroes of the occasion." [394]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Arafat wuquf ♥ The key ritual within the wider sequence of rituals that makes up the Hajj. “On the morning of the ninth, [pilgrims] proceed towards ‘Arafat, a plain 14.5km (nine miles) from Mina, where the central rite of the Hajj, wuquf (standing) on ‘Arafat, takes place between noon and sunset, during which time many heartfelt prayers are offered. The scene reminds Muslims of the gathering on the Day of Judgement. If any pilgrim misses this event, his/her Hajj is not valid and has to be done again another year. This does not apply to any other rite, all of which can be done over a longer period or compensated for with an offering. This is the time for the pilgrims to read the Qur’an, glorify God and pray most earnestly for forgiveness and everything else they wish for. Particularly recommended for glorification and repetition is this prayer, ‘There is no God but Allah alone, with no partner. Dominion and praise belong to Him, who gives life and death. Goodness is in His hands and He has power over everything.’ This just gives a succinct summary of the Islamic faith and connects the individual pilgrim to what Ibrahim and Muhammad brought and uttered. Pilgrims normally face towards the Ka‘ba while reciting these prayers, as they do for the five daily prayers, believing that, by doing this, their prayers are more likely to be accepted.” [404]. This ritual is coded here as dysphoric due to the fact that pilgrims are required to stand for several hours (between noon and sunset) under the sun’s heat. A contemporary account, dating to 1997, notes that the “heat is virtually unbearable” [405]. However, it is worth noting that, due to peculiarities of the Islamic calendar, this ritual may sometimes be performed at a time of the year when the temperature is less hot than at other times, and when there are fewer others between noon and sunset than at other times. Moreover, a few informal online sources suggest that those who cannot stand in the sun for the duration of the ritual are allowed to be in the shade and/or sit down. Still, though relatively mild, this appears to be the most dysphoric ritual within Sunni Islam.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar [406].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[407]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 6 ♥ hours, on average. The ritual takes place between noon and sunset.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all Muslims[408]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ inferred present ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [409], but, because participants must devote all their energies in prayer, it seems likely that they were not allowed to eat. Then again, this may not count as fasting due to the relative brevity of the ritual (between 5 and 7 hours, depending on the time of the year when it is performed on any given year).
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ absent ♥ Though the ritual is a vigil, it takes place during the daytime [410].
♠ fear ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [411].

♠ animal sacrifice ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [436].
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”

[437]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ ♥
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ ♥
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 13 ♥ 0. Hulagu (1256-1265 CE)[440] 1. Abaqa (1265-1281 CE)[441] 2. Teguder (1282-1284 CE)[442] 3. Arghun (1284-1291 CE)[443] 4. Gaykhatu (1291-1295 CE)[444] 5. Baydu (1295 CE)[445] 6. Ghazan (1295-1304 CE)[446] 7. Uljaytu (1304-1317 CE)[447] 8. Abu Sa'id (1317-1335 CE)[448] 9. Arpa Khan (1335-1336 CE)[449] ***10. Musa (1336)[450] ***11. Mohammad Shah (1337)[451] ***12. Queen Sati Beg (1338-1339)[452] ***13. Sulayman Khan (1339 - now Iran splits into four kingdoms)[453] The chronology in 'The Oxford Handbook of Iranian History' presumably for simplicity omits the breakdown succession calling it "disunited 1336-"[454]. However the narrative of events suggests there was a short succession of rulers. If this period of instability occurred during the middle of the polity we would include them. So we must include them when the period of instability occurs at the end. *** Confirmed: Morby's reference ends with the split between Sulayman and Jahan Temur in 1339 CE.[455]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 9 ♥ *1265* No transition contest Abaqa: "the unity and stability of the new regime began to unravel after Abaqa's death ... princes and bureaucratic clans began to destroy what order and discipline Hulegu and his son had succeeded in implementing..."[456] *1282* Teguder: "Abagha had wanted his son Arghun ... to take over, but Teguder prevented that. Teguder converted to Islam. Then he tried to establish an alliance with the Muslims of Egypt. But before he could make the Ilkhanate an Islamic state, Teguder was assassinated by Arghun and some Mongol princes loyal to him."[457] *1284* Inferred present Arghun: At the beginning of Arghun's reign the members of the previous regime were purged in what one Mongol historian called a 'bloodbath'.[458] *1291* No transition contest Gaykhatu: "Within four years, rebels led by Baidu ... a grandson of Hulegu, strangled Geikhatu."[459] *1295* Baydu: "Almost immediately, Arghun's son Ghazan ... challenged Baidu's authority. Although once a Buddhist, Ghazan followed the advice of one of his generals and converted to Islam. ... By the end of 1295, Ghazan had won enough support to force out Baidu and become the next Ilkhan."[460] *1295* No transition contest Ghazan: No mention of a transition contest with the accession of Ghazan.[461][462] *1304* Uljaytu: "Even before Uljaytu was crowned, it was deemed expedient to get rid of his cousin Alafrank as a possible claimant to the throne, and he, as well as the general Harqadaq, was accordingly assassinated by three Mongol officers."[463] *1317* inferred present Abu Sa'id: The start of the reign of "Abu Sa'id, the son and successor of Uljaytu" was highly unstable but there is no explicit mention of a challenge to his position: "His surviving son, Abu Sa'id, succeeded him ... crowned in April, 1317, being then under thirteen years of age. ... Between these two ministers there existed a great rivalry ... The victim was Rashidud-Din ... By the intrigues of his rival he was deposed ... (August, 1318) began the rebellion of Yasawur, whose ambition led him to covet the province of Khurasan. ... About the same time a formidable conspiracy of Mongol captains ... but the latter, supported by Abu Sa'id, utterly defeated them..."[464] However, the 'conspiracy of Mongol captains' gets very close and it happens about a year after the beginning of his reign. Given the instability this probably ought to count as a contested transition on the basis the contest began within the first year of his reign. *1335* Arpa Khan: "On the death of Abu Sa'id, who left no sons, Arpa, or Arpaga'un, a descendant of Arik-buqa, the brother of Hulagu, was, at the instance of the minister Ghiyathu'd-Din b. Rashidu'd-Din, chosen as his successor. To strengthen his position he married Sati Beg, the widow of Chuban and sister of Abu Sa'id. He then marched against Uzbek and defeated him. But meanwhile Amir Ali Padishah and other amirs, disapproving of Arpa's election, set up a rival Il-khan in the person of Musa, a descendant of Hulagu. A battle took place between the two rivals near Maragha on April 29, 1336. Arpa was defeated, and both he and the wazir Ghiyathu'd-Din were put to death shortly afterwards."[465] *1336* Musa: "Musa, however, was not suffered to enjoy the fruits of victory for long: another rival, Muhammad Shah, also descended from Hulagu, was set up against him by Shaykh Hasan the Jala'ir (called Buzurg, 'the Great'). Another battle was fought at Ala-Tagh near the town of Naw-Shahr, in which, by the treachery of Shaykh Hasan Buzurg, Musa was routed and Ali Padishah killed. Yet another claimant was set up in the person of Tughay-Timur, who joined forces with Musa, and fought another battle with Shaykh Hasan Buzurg near Maragha in June 1227, in which Musa was taken prisoner and put to death (July, 1337), while Tughay-Timur fled to Bistam."[466] *1337* Muhammad Shah: "Shaykh Hasan, the son of Timur-Tash, the son of Chuban, now added to the confusion by producing a pretender whom he asserted to be his father Timur-Tash, whose execution by the Sultan of Egypt has been already mentioned. A battle finally took place at Nakhjuwan on July 10, 1338, between the two Hasans, in which Hasan 'the Greater' was defeated, while his protege Muhammad Shah was taken prisoner and put to death. Shaykh Hasan 'the Less' (the grandson of Chuban) now quarrelled with the princess Sati Beg, the sister of the late king Abu Sa'id and widow of his grandfather Chuban. She was proclaimed queen in 739 (1338-9), and a reconciliation was effected between the two Hasans."[467] *1338* Queen Sati Beg: "It is hardly worth following these intrigues further. ... Suffice it to say that Tughay-Timur was betrayed by the astute Shaykh Hasan ... who then set up another puppet, Sulayman Khan, a descendant of Hulagu, and gave him Sati Beg in marriage,"[468] *1339* Sulayman Khan: "who then set up another puppet, Sulayman Khan, a descendant of Hulagu, and gave him Sati Beg in marriage, while Hasan 'the Greater' set up as a rival a descendant of Abaqa named Shah Jahan Timur. A battle took place ... 1340 ... Hasan 'the Greater' was defeated ... deposed his puppet ... proclaiming himself king founded the dynasty ... of the Jala'irs, who reigned until 1411 over Western Persia and Mesopotamia with Baghdad as their capital. As for Hasan 'the Less,' ... he was murdered in 1343, while marching to attack his rival, by his wife ... The Mongol ascendancy in Persia was now at an end, and, until Timur's hordes swept over the country (1384-1393), it was divided into at least four kingdoms, those of the Jala'irs, the Muzaffaris, the Kurts, and the Sar-ba-dars..."[469]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 5 ♥ *1295* Baydu: "Almost immediately, Arghun's son Ghazan ... challenged Baidu's authority. ♠ State salary ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Amitai noted, however, that in the absence of a salary, the nomadic sector began to feel financial pressure as booty from conquest diminished, making it harder and harder to survive in the conquered Iranian lands."[477] Yet they had indigenous coinage[478][479] - so how did they use it? Did anyone get paid in coinage?
♠ Governed population ♣ present ♥ "Neither in the aftermath of the Mongol conquest nor in the early years of the Ilkhanate was the combined salary and tax-farm system known as iqta' employed in Iran. ... The iqta system was widely used and abused during the Saljuq period but in the early Mongol/Ilkhanate period it seems to have been limited to 'soldiers from pre-Mongol regimes who continued to serve the Mongols, as in the case of Khwarazmian troops.' ... late in his reign, Ghazan reintroduced the ancient system of iqta' for the military in those areas where the summer and winter pastures of the tumens lay (i.e., in the north), the traditional grazing grounds of the Mongol and Turkic tribes that had come with them, with fewer such grants in Fars and Kerman."[480] "Qazwini in Zafarnama (published c1334/5) estimated 50,000 Mongols were supported by iqta'."[481]
♠ Land ♣ present ♥ Senior officials were granted land holdings to support their offices.[482][483]

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ inferred present ♥ "Rashid al-Din Fadl Allah, Ghazan Khan's wazir, gives an extremely unfavourable account of the administration of justice by the kadis in the Ilkhan empire prior to the reign of Ghazan Kahn (as he does of other aspects of the administration). He alleges that corrupt and ignorant persons insinuated themselves into the service of the Mongols and by flattery and bribery secured the office of kadi and other shar'i offices. Corruption was especially prevalent in transactions over landed property."[484]
♠ Bribery ♣ inferred present ♥ "Rashid al-Din Fadl Allah, Ghazan Khan's wazir, gives an extremely unfavourable account of the administration of justice by the kadis in the Ilkhan empire prior to the reign of Ghazan Kahn (as he does of other aspects of the administration). Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ ♥

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ ♥

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ inferred present ♥ Islamic law was acknowledged by the Khans, and Islamic judges were regulated under Ghazan's administrative reforms.[488] Taken from a modern textbook (were historical interpretations different?) under Islamic Sharia the defendant has the following rights: to withdraw a forced confession; to a fair trial; an independent and impartial judge; equal protection under law; of representation by a lawyer; right to question evidence; right to public trial; right to judgement 'within a reasonable time'; that 'his guilt must be established without a reasonable doubt'".[489]
